   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Minor-planet moon
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">

          </li>

      <p>
       A
       <b>
        minor-planet moon
       </b>
       is an
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_object" title="Astronomical object">
        astronomical object
       </a>
       that
       <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">
        orbits
       </a>
       a
       <a href="/wiki/Minor_planet" title="Minor planet">
        minor planet
       </a>
       as its
       <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">
        natural satellite
       </a>
       . It is thought that many
       <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">
        asteroids
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">
        Kuiper belt
       </a>
       objects may possess moons, in some cases quite substantial in size. Discoveries of minor-planet moons (and binary objects, in general) are important because the determination of their orbits provides estimates on the
       <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">
        mass
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">
        density
       </a>
       of the primary, allowing insights of their physical properties that is generally not otherwise possible.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SwRI2000_1-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SwRI2000-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The first modern era mention of the possibility of an asteroid satellite was in connection with an occultation of the bright star Gamma Ceti by the minor planet
       <a href="/wiki/6_Hebe" title="6 Hebe">
        (6) Hebe
       </a>
       in 1977. The observer, amateur astronomer Paul D. Maley, detected an unmistakable 0.5 second disappearance of this naked eye star from a site near
       <a href="/wiki/Victoria,_Texas" title="Victoria, Texas">
        Victoria, Texas
       </a>
       . Many hours later, several observations were reported in Mexico attributed to the occultation by (6) Hebe itself. Although not confirmed this documents the first formally documented case of a suspected companion of an asteroid.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As of October 2016, there are over 300 minor planets known to have moons.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnston2008_3-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Johnston2008-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Terminology">
        Terminology
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Minor-planet_moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Terminology">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Binary_asteroid" title="Binary asteroid">
        Binary asteroid
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       In addition to the terms
       <i>
        satellite
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        moon
       </i>
       , the term "binary" (
       <b>
        binary minor planet
       </b>
       ) is sometimes used for minor planets with moons, and "triple" for minor planets with two moons. If one object is much bigger it can be referred to as the
       <i>
        primary
       </i>
       and its companion as
       <i>
        secondary
       </i>
       . The term
       <i>
        double asteroid
       </i>
       is sometimes used for systems in which the asteroid and its moon are roughly the same size, while
       <i>
        binary
       </i>
       tends to be used independently from the relative sizes of the components. When binary minor planets are similar in size, the
       <a href="/wiki/Minor_Planet_Center" title="Minor Planet Center">
        Minor Planet Center
       </a>
       (MPC) refers to them as "
       <a href="/wiki/Binary_asteroid" title="Binary asteroid">
        binary companions
       </a>
       " instead of referring to the smaller body as a satellite.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAU-list_4-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IAU-list-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A good example of a true binary is the
       <a href="/wiki/90_Antiope" title="90 Antiope">
        90 Antiope
       </a>
       system, identified in August 2000.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keck2000_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Keck2000-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Small satellites are often referred to as moonlets.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SwRI2000_1-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-SwRI2000-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAUC_8732_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IAUC_8732-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_milestones">
        Discovery milestones
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Minor-planet_moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Discovery milestones">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       As of February 2017, over 330 moons of minor planets have been discovered.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnston2008_3-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Johnston2008-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These consist of:
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        63 around
        <a href="/wiki/Near-Earth_object" title="Near-Earth object">
         near-Earth objects
        </a>
        ,
        <ul>
         <li>
          Two with two satellites:
          <span class="nowrap">
           <a href="/wiki/(136617)_1994_CC" title="(136617) 1994 CC">
            (136617) 1994 CC
           </a>
          </span>
          and
          <span class="nowrap">
           <a href="/wiki/(153591)_2001_SN263" title="(153591) 2001 SN263">
            (153591) 2001 SN
            <span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;">
             <span style="font-size:80%;">
              263
             </span>
            </span>
           </a>
          </span>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        23 orbiting
        <a href="/wiki/List_of_Mars-crossing_minor_planets" title="List of Mars-crossing minor planets">
         Mars-crossing asteroids
        </a>
        ,
        <ul>
         <li>
          One with two satellites:
          <a href="/wiki/2577_Litva" title="2577 Litva">
           2577 Litva
          </a>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        143 in the
        <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">
         asteroid belt
        </a>
        ,
        <ul>
         <li>
          Seven with two satellites:
          <a href="/wiki/45_Eugenia" title="45 Eugenia">
           45 Eugenia
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/87_Sylvia" title="87 Sylvia">
           87 Sylvia
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/93_Minerva" title="93 Minerva">
           93 Minerva
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/107_Camilla" title="107 Camilla">
           107 Camilla
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/130_Elektra" title="130 Elektra">
           130 Elektra
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/216_Kleopatra" title="216 Kleopatra">
           216 Kleopatra
          </a>
          , and
          <a href="/wiki/3749_Balam" title="3749 Balam">
           3749 Balam
          </a>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        18 around
        <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_trojan" title="Jupiter trojan">
         Jupiter trojans
        </a>
        ,
       </li>
       <li>
        2 around
        <a href="/wiki/Centaur_(minor_planet)" title="Centaur (minor planet)">
         centaurs
        </a>
        (both in the form of ring systems that are likely contained by
        <a href="/wiki/Shepherd_moon" title="Shepherd moon">
         shepherd moons
        </a>
        ), and
       </li>
       <li>
        87 moons of
        <a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">
         trans-Neptunian objects
        </a>
        <ul>
         <li>
          Two with two satellites:
          <a href="/wiki/47171_Lempo" title="47171 Lempo">
           47171 Lempo
          </a>
          and
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/136108_Haumea" title="136108 Haumea">
           136108 Haumea
          </a>
         </li>
         <li>
          One with five satellites:
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/134340_Pluto" title="134340 Pluto">
           134340 Pluto
          </a>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnston2008_3-2">
           <a href="#cite_note-Johnston2008-3">
            [3]
           </a>
          </sup>
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marchis2008_7-0">
           <a href="#cite_note-Marchis2008-7">
            [7]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       Prior to the era of the
       <i>
        Hubble Space Telescope
       </i>
       and space probes reaching the outer Solar System, attempts to detect satellites around asteroids were limited to optical observations from Earth. For example, in 1978,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asteroid_occultation" title="Asteroid occultation">
        stellar occultation
       </a>
       observations were claimed as evidence of a satellite for the
       <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">
        asteroid
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/532_Herculina" title="532 Herculina">
        532 Herculina
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, later more-detailed imaging by the Hubble Telescope did not reveal a satellite, and the current consensus is that Herculina does not have a significant satellite.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Storrs1999_9-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Storrs1999-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       There were other similar reports of asteroids having companions (usually referred to as satellites) in the following years. Also, a letter in
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Sky_%26_Telescope" title="Sky &amp; Telescope">
         Sky &amp; Telescope
        </a>
       </i>
       magazine
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2013)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       at this time pointed to apparently simultaneous impact craters on Earth (for example, the
       <a href="/wiki/Clearwater_Lakes" title="Clearwater Lakes">
        Clearwater Lakes
       </a>
       in Quebec), suggesting that these craters were caused by pairs of gravitationally-bound objects.
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1993, the first asteroid moon was confirmed when the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galileo_probe" title="Galileo probe">
        Galileo probe
       </a>
       discovered the small
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dactyl_(moon)" title="Dactyl (moon)">
        Dactyl
       </a>
       orbiting
       <a href="/wiki/243_Ida" title="243 Ida">
        243 Ida
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">
        asteroid belt
       </a>
       . The second was discovered around
       <a href="/wiki/45_Eugenia" title="45 Eugenia">
        45 Eugenia
       </a>
       in 1998.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eugenia_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Eugenia-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2001,
       <a href="/wiki/617_Patroclus" title="617 Patroclus">
        617 Patroclus
       </a>
       and its same-sized companion Menoetius became the first known binary asteroids in the
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_trojan" title="Jupiter trojan">
        Jupiter trojans
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merline_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Merline-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The first
       <a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">
        trans-Neptunian
       </a>
       binary after Pluto–Charon,
       <span class="nowrap">
        <a href="/wiki/1998_WW31" title="1998 WW31">
         1998 WW
         <span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;">
          <span style="font-size:80%;">
           31
          </span>
         </span>
        </a>
       </span>
       , was optically resolved in 2002.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chiang2006_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chiang2006-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Triple_systems">
        Triple systems
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Minor-planet_moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Triple systems">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Triple minor planets, or
       <b>
        trinary minor planets
       </b>
       , are known since 2005, when the asteroid
       <a href="/wiki/87_Sylvia" title="87 Sylvia">
        87 Sylvia
       </a>
       was discovered to have two satellites, making it the first known triple system.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAUC8582_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IAUC8582-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This was followed by the discovery of a second moon orbiting
       <a href="/wiki/45_Eugenia" title="45 Eugenia">
        45 Eugenia
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IAUC8817_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-IAUC8817-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Also in 2005, the dwarf planet
       <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">
        Haumea
       </a>
       was discovered to have two moons, making it the second trans-Neptunian object after
       <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">
        Pluto
       </a>
       known to have more than one moon.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marchishomepage_15-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Marchishomepage-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Additionally,
       <a href="/wiki/216_Kleopatra" title="216 Kleopatra">
        216 Kleopatra
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SETI_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SETI-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/93_Minerva" title="93 Minerva">
        93 Minerva
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marchis2009_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Marchis2009-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       were discovered to be trinary asteroids in 2008 and 2009 respectively. Since the first few triple minor planets were discovered, more continue to be discovered at a rate of about one a year. Most recently discovered was a third moon orbiting the asteroid
       <a href="/wiki/107_Camilla" title="107 Camilla">
        107 Camilla
       </a>
       , bringing the number of known trinary systems in the
       <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">
        asteroid belt
       </a>
       up to 7. The following table lists all satellites of these systems chronologically by their discovery date, starting with
       <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">
        Charon
       </a>
       , discovered in 1978.
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Commonality">
        Commonality
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Minor-planet_moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Commonality">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The data about the populations of binary objects are still patchy. In addition to the inevitable observational bias (dependence on the distance from Earth, size,
       <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">
        albedo
       </a>
       and separation of the components) the frequency appears to be different among different categories of objects. Among asteroids, an estimated 2% would have satellites. Among
       <a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">
        trans-Neptunian objects
       </a>
       (TNO), an estimated 11% are thought to be binary or multiple objects, and the majority of the large TNOs have at least one satellite, including all four IAU-listed dwarf planets.
      </p>
      <p>
       More than 50 binaries are known in each of the main groupings:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near_Earth_asteroid" title="Near Earth asteroid">
        near-Earth
       </a>
       asteroids,
       <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">
        belt asteroids
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_objects" title="Trans-Neptunian objects">
        trans-Neptunian objects
       </a>
       , not including numerous claims based solely on light-curve variation.
      </p>
      <p>
       Two binaries have been found so far among
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centaur_(planetoid)" title="Centaur (planetoid)">
        centaurs
       </a>
       with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">
        semi-major axes
       </a>
       smaller than Neptune.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Noll2005_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Noll2005-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Both are double ring systems around
       <a href="/wiki/2060_Chiron" title="2060 Chiron">
        2060 Chiron
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/10199_Chariklo" title="10199 Chariklo">
        10199 Chariklo
       </a>
       , discovered in 1994–2011 and 2013 respectively.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">
        Origin
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Minor-planet_moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Origin">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The origin of minor-planet moons is not currently known with certainty, and a variety of theories exist. A widely accepted theory is that minor-planet moons are formed from debris knocked off of the primary by an impact. Other pairings may be formed when a small object is captured by the gravity of a larger one.
      </p>
      <p>
       Formation by collision is constrained by the
       <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">
        angular momentum
       </a>
       of the components, i.e. by the masses and their separation. Close binaries fit this model (e.g.
       <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">
        Pluto
       </a>
       –
       <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">
        Charon
       </a>
       ). Distant binaries however, with components of comparable size, are unlikely to have followed this scenario, unless considerable mass has been lost in the event.
      </p>
      <p>
       The distances of the components for the known binaries vary from a few hundreds of kilometres (
       <a href="/wiki/243_Ida" title="243 Ida">
        243 Ida
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/3749_Balam" title="3749 Balam">
        3749 Balam
       </a>
       ) to more than 3000 km (
       <a href="/wiki/379_Huenna" title="379 Huenna">
        379 Huenna
       </a>
       ) for the asteroids. Among TNOs, the known separations vary from 3,000 to 50,000 km.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Noll2005_18-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Noll2005-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Populations_and_classes">
        Populations and classes
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Minor-planet_moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Populations and classes">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       What is "typical" for a binary system tends to depend on its location in the Solar System (presumably because of different modes of origin and lifetimes of such systems in different populations of minor planets).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MicBarVel04_19-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MicBarVel04-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Among
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near-Earth_asteroid" title="Near-Earth asteroid">
          near-Earth asteroids
         </a>
        </i>
        , satellites tend to orbit at distances of the order of 3–7 primary radii, and have diameters two to several times smaller than the primary. Since these binaries are all inner-planet crossers, it is thought that tidal stresses that occurred when the parent object passed close to a planet may be responsible for the formation of many of them, although collisions are thought to also be a factor in the creation of these satellites.
       </li>
       <li>
        Among
        <i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Main-belt" title="Main-belt">
          main-belt
         </a>
         <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">
          asteroids
         </a>
        </i>
        , the satellites are usually much smaller than the primary (a notable exception being
        <a href="/wiki/90_Antiope" title="90 Antiope">
         90 Antiope
        </a>
        ), and orbit around 10 primary radii away. Many of the binary systems here are members of
        <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_family" title="Asteroid family">
         asteroid families
        </a>
        , and a good proportion of satellites are expected to be fragments of a parent body whose disruption after an asteroid collision produced both the primary and satellite.
       </li>
       <li>
        Among
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">
          trans-Neptunian objects
         </a>
        </i>
        , it is common for the two orbiting components to be of comparable size, and for the semi-major axis of their orbits to be much larger − about 100 to 1000 primary radii. A significant proportion of these binaries are expected to be primordial.
       </li>
       <li>
        Among the
        <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">
         dwarf planets
        </a>
        , it is 90 percent certain that
        <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">
         Ceres
        </a>
        has no moons larger than 1 km in size, assuming that they would have the same
        <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">
         albedo
        </a>
        as Ceres itself.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ceres_no_moons_20-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-ceres_no_moons-20">
          [20]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">
           Pluto
          </a>
          has five known moons. Its largest moon
          <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">
           Charon
          </a>
          is more than half the size of Pluto itself, and large enough to orbit a point outside Pluto's surface. In fact, each orbits the common
          <a href="/wiki/Center_of_mass#Barycenter_in_astrophysics_and_astronomy" title="Center of mass">
           barycenter
          </a>
          between them, with Pluto's orbit entirely enclosed by Charon's; thus they form a
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binary_system_(astronomy)" title="Binary system (astronomy)">
           binary system
          </a>
          informally referred to as a
          <a href="/wiki/Double_planet" title="Double planet">
           double dwarf planet
          </a>
          . Pluto's four other moons,
          <a href="/wiki/Nix_(moon)" title="Nix (moon)">
           Nix
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Hydra_(moon)" title="Hydra (moon)">
           Hydra
          </a>
          ,
          <a href="/wiki/Kerberos_(moon)" title="Kerberos (moon)">
           Kerberos
          </a>
          , and
          <a href="/wiki/Styx_(moon)" title="Styx (moon)">
           Styx
          </a>
          , are far smaller and orbit the Pluto–Charon system.
         </li>
         <li>
          <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">
           Haumea
          </a>
          has two moons with radii estimated around 155 km (
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hi%27iaka_(moon)" title="Hi'iaka (moon)">
           Hiʻiaka
          </a>
          ) and 85 km (
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Namaka_(moon)" title="Namaka (moon)">
           Namaka
          </a>
          ).
         </li>
         <li>
          <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">
           Makemake
          </a>
          has one known moon,
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S/2015_(136472)_1" title="S/2015 (136472) 1">
           S/2015 (136472) 1
          </a>
          , estimated to be some 160 kilometers (100 mi) in diameter.
         </li>
         <li>
          <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">
           Eris
          </a>
          has one known moon,
          <a href="/wiki/Dysnomia_(moon)" title="Dysnomia (moon)">
           Dysnomia
          </a>
          . Its radius, based on its brightness, is estimated to be roughly between 150 and 350 km.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TNOCool4_21-0">
           <a href="#cite_note-TNOCool4-21">
            [21]
           </a>
          </sup>
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_minor_planets_with_moons">
        List of minor planets with moons
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Minor-planet_moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: List of minor planets with moons">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Near-Earth_objects">
        Near-Earth objects
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Minor-planet_moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Near-Earth objects">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       There are 62 known
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near-Earth_asteroid" title="Near-Earth asteroid">
        near-Earth asteroids
       </a>
       with moons (17
       <a href="/wiki/Amor_asteroid" title="Amor asteroid">
        Amor
       </a>
       type, 37
       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_asteroid" title="Apollo asteroid">
        Apollo
       </a>
       type, 7
       <a href="/wiki/Aten_asteroid" title="Aten asteroid">
        Aten
       </a>
       type, and 1
       <a href="/wiki/Atira_asteroid" title="Atira asteroid">
        Atira
       </a>
       type) with a total of 64 moons.
      </p>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mars_crossers">
        Mars crossers
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Minor-planet_moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Mars crossers">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       There are 22
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mars-crossing_asteroid" title="Mars-crossing asteroid">
        Mars-crossing asteroids
       </a>
       with moons with a total of 23 moons.

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Asteroid_belt">
        Asteroid belt
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Minor-planet_moon&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Asteroid belt">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       There are 136 asteroids with 143 moons in the
       <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">
        asteroid belt
       </a>
       . Asteroids with light blue backgrounds are double asteroids, with similarly-sized components, and a
       <a href="/wiki/Barycenter" title="Barycenter">
        barycenter
       </a>
       outside of the larger object. very light blue asteroids might be double asteroids, but due to errors in their size and orbit, it is uncertain.
      </p>
      <table class="wikitable plainrowheaders sortable" style="text-align: center;">
       <tr>
        <th scope="col">

          Diameter
         </a>
         (km)
         <br/>
         (or dimensions)
        </th>
        <th scope="col" width="110">
         Name of moon
        </th>
        <th scope="col">
         Diameter of moon (km)
         <br/>
         (or dimensions)
        </th>
        <th scope="col">
         Separation (km)
        </th>
        <th scope="col">

        </th>

         (m from
         <br/>
         center of primary)
        </th>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Centaurs">
        Centaurs
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">

         edit
        </a>

         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       There are only 2
       <a href="/wiki/Centaur_(minor_planet)" title="Centaur (minor planet)">

       </a>

          </a>
         </i>
